[1mูุฑุงุกุงุช ุฅุถุงููุฉ [0m

There have been many books written about plagues and in particular about the Black Death, but most promulgate the theory that 
bubonic plague was responsible or are concerned with the social consequences of the pandemic. 

Twigg, G. (1984) [3mThe Black Death: A Biological Appraisal [0m, London: Batsford Academic. This was the first book to assemble the 
evidence and to show convincingly that bubonic plague was not responsible for the Black Death. 

Shrewsbury, J. F. D. (1970) [3mA History of Bubonic Plague in the British Isles [0m, Cambridge: Cambridge University Press. This gives a 
comprehensive account of the epidemics in England, although it is a rather boring read. Shrewsbury believed whole-heartedly that [3m
Yersinia pestis [0mwas responsible for the pestilence but, as a medical microbiologist, realized that this was an impossibility in 
many of the epidemics. His repeated attempts to rationalize the situation spoil an important compendium. A must if you want to 
find out about recorded plague epidemics in your local area. 

We have presented a new, scientific and mathematical approach to the epidemiology in Scott, S. and Duncan, C. J. (2001) [3mBiology of 
Plagues: Evidence from Historical Populations [0m, Cambridge: Cambridge University Press. 

McNeill, W. H. (1977) [3mPlagues and People [0m, Oxford: Blackwell gives a general account. 

An account of events during the arrival of the Great Pestilence is given in Ziegler, P. (1969) [3mThe Black Death [0m, London: Collins. 

Biraben, J. N. (1975) [3mLes hommes et la peste en France et dans les pays Europรฉens et Mรฉditerraneens, Vols 1 and 2 [0m, Paris: Mouton 
& Co and รcole des Hautes รtudes en Sciences Sociales. An invaluable compilation of the recorded plagues in Europe, listed by 
geographic area. There is a comprehensive bibliography. Biraben also gives an authoritative account of true bubonic plague at 
Marseille in 1720โ22. 

Pepys, S. (1665โ1906) [3mDiary of Samuel Pepys [0m, London: J. M. Dent. The Great Plague of London in 1665-66 is the best documented of 
all the epidemics in Europe. Samuel Pepys stayed in London throughout and went about his daily business. His diary is lively and 
provides an invaluable eye-witness account. 

Defoe, D. (1722) [3mDiary of a Plague Year [0m, London: Everymanโs Library. Daniel Defoe was six in 1665 and did not write his very 
readable story of the Great Plague until 1722. It has been criticized as being fictional, but checking against contemporary 
sources suggests that this is an accurate account of life during one of the terrible epidemics. 

Bell, W. G. (1924) [3mThe Great Plague in London in 1665 [0m, London: John Lane. Probably the definitive account. 

Creighton, C. (1894) [3mHistory of Epidemics in Britain [0m, Cambridge: Cambridge University Press. Creighton is the doyen of 
epidemiologists. Although medically qualified, his approach in this classic work, which was to provide a chronicle of death and 
disease in the people of the UK, was that of a professional historian and he worked with great care on his sources. 

An account of the epidemic at Eyam published 30 years before Yersinโs work on bubonic plague is given by Wood, W. (1865) [3mThe 
History of Antiquities of Eyam [0m, 4th edn, London: Bell and Daldy. It contains no confusing mention of rats and fleas. 

The plagues of Iceland are of particular interest because they were constrained within this isolated island community and were 
initiated by the arrival of a single ship. Karlsson, G. (1996) โPlague without rats: the case of fifteenth century Icelandโ, [3m
Journal of Medieval History, [0m22: 263โ84. It would be particularly interesting to measure the frequency of the CCR5-ฮ32 mutation in 
Iceland today. 

An erudite account of plagues in the early Islamic Empire is Dols, M. W. (1977) [3mThe Black Death in the Middle East [0m, Princeton, 
NJ: Princeton University Press. 

The effect of the plagues in controlling the demography of Europe is covered in two of our books: Scott, S. and Duncan, C. J. 
(1998) [3mHuman Demography and Disease [0m, Cambridge: Cambridge University Press, and Scott S. and Duncan, C. J. (2002) [3mDemography and 
Nutrition [0m, Oxford: Blackwell. 

There are many papers or books that cover specialized aspects of our story, for example: Davis, D. E. (1986) โThe scarcity of rats 
and the Black Death: An ecological historyโ, [3mJournal of Interdisciplinary History, [0mXVI: 455โ70. 

Dyer, A. (1997) โThe English Sweating Sickness of 1551: An epidemic anatomizedโ, [3mMedical History, [0m41: 362โ84. 

Taviner, M., Thwaites, G. and Grant, V. (1998) โThe English Sweating Sickness, 1485โ1551: A viral pulmonary disease?โ [3mMedical 
History [0m, 42: 96โ8. 

Furness, W. (1894) [3mThe History of Penrith from the Earliest Period to the Present Time by Ewanian (William Furness) [0m, Penrith: 
William Furness. 

Hughes, J. (1971) โThe plague at Carlisle 1597/8โ, [3mTransactions of the Cumberland and Westmorland Antiquarian and Archaeological 
Society [0m, 81: 52โ63. 

Longrigg, J. (1980) โThe great plague of Athensโ, [3mHistory of Science [0m, 18: 209โ25. 

Pfister, C. (1980) โThe Little Ice Age: Thermal and wetness indices for Central Europeโ, [3mJournal of Interdisciplinary History, [0m10: 
665โ96. 

Twigg, G. (1978) โThe role of rodents in plague transmission: A worldwide reviewโ, [3mMammal Review, [0m8: 77โ110. 

Twigg, G. (2003) โThe Black Death: A problem of population-wide infectionโ, [3mLocal Population Studies, [0m71: 40โ52. Covers evidence 
that the black rat was not present in rural England during the plague. 

A detailed account of the array of newly emerging diseases in a world out of balance is given by Garrett, L. (1994) [3mThe Coming 
Plague [0m, New York: Penguin Books. 

Miller J., Engelberg, S. and Broad, W. (2001) [3mGerms: The Ultimate Weapon [0m, New York: Simon & Schuster. Comprehensive coverage of 
biological weapons of mass destruction and current fears of bioterrorism.
